  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_c5e5cd3b-79f4-49c5-a316-3dbb80016da5'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

  <span id="freeText13416122079987091667" style="display:none"><i>'Sorry?' said Carrot. If it's just a thing, how can it commit murder? A sword is a thing' - he drew his own sword; it made an almost silken sound - 'and of course you can't blame a sword if someone thrust it at you, sir.'</i><br /><br />For members of the City Watch, life consists of troubling times, linked together by periods of torpid inactivity. Now is one such troubling time. People are being murdered, but there's no trace of anything alive having been at the crime scene. Is there ever a circumstance in which you can blame the weapon not the murderer? Such philosophical questions are not the usual domain of the city's police, but they're going to have to start learning fast...</span>

<div id="review_61530023" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/61530023" />
    <a title="Patrick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/922495-patrick"><img alt="Patrick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1351307095p2/922495.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/61530023?book_show_action=true">Jun 29, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patrick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patrick" href="/user/show/922495-patrick">Patrick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/922495-patrick?shelf=books-i-would-blurb">books-i-would-blurb</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer61530023" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10023204820206572336">In my opinion, this is the book where Pratchett *really* hits his stride in terms of the city watch books. The characters are established, the setting is solid, and Pratchett is solidly in control of his craft here.<br/><br/>As I said before (or at least meant to say) the second book about the city watch was twice as good as the first. Similarly, this book is twice as good as the second one. Putting it solidly in the familiar A+ quality book that comprises easily half of Pratchett's work.<br/><br/>Of personal inter</span>
  <span id="freeText10023204820206572336" style="display:none">In my opinion, this is the book where Pratchett *really* hits his stride in terms of the city watch books. The characters are established, the setting is solid, and Pratchett is solidly in control of his craft here.<br /><br />As I said before (or at least meant to say) the second book about the city watch was twice as good as the first. Similarly, this book is twice as good as the second one. Putting it solidly in the familiar A+ quality book that comprises easily half of Pratchett's work.<br /><br />Of personal interest to me is the introduction of the Golem into the Discworld. In previous books, Pratchett has talked about issues of discrimination, but the Golems allow him to broaden the subject into a much larger discussion along the lines of "What makes a person a person?" and perhaps more importantly "What sort of person should a person be?"<br /> <br />It's also interesting to note the appearance of Buggy Squires who is called a Gnome. But for those who have read the entire series, he's obviously a precursor to the Nac Mac Feagles that eventually appear in the Tiffany aching books.<br /><br />As an author, it's cool for me to see how that concept peeked its head up in Pratchett's work almost 10 years before it became fully realized in a much later book.... <br /><br />Is this book worth your time? Yes. A thousand times yes.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10023204820206572336" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2320525579" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2320525579" />
    <a title="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus"><img alt="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588320656p2/78485297.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2320525579?book_show_action=true">Mar 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " href="/user/show/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus">Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus?shelf=pratchett-terry">pratchett-terry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2320525579" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11035788748418663130">Golem slavery surrounding a conspiracy investigated by special City Watch forensic experts while a silent revolution takes place.<br/><br/>Golems, Hephaistos´ machines, and similar mythological stuff are examples of ancient storytellers dealing with the always current „They are stealing our jobs“ problem. Depending on genre preference, it can be seen as a fantastic ability to give life to dead objects, an alien ability to give technology to primitive humans, or be welcomed as the option that humans alread</span>
  <span id="freeText11035788748418663130" style="display:none">Golem slavery surrounding a conspiracy investigated by special City Watch forensic experts while a silent revolution takes place.<br /><br />Golems, Hephaistos´ machines, and similar mythological stuff are examples of ancient storytellers dealing with the always current „They are stealing our jobs“ problem. Depending on genre preference, it can be seen as a fantastic ability to give life to dead objects, an alien ability to give technology to primitive humans, or be welcomed as the option that humans already had sophisticated technology, but ruined everything. But to stay on Earth, one may prefer the boring option that human creativity thought up much of the nano, robot, and technical revolution before there even was a smallest piece of modern technology available to degenerate with.<br /><br />Indirect, subtle slavery, cemented with the argument that the victims are too primitive to understand their mistreatment and exploitation, is nothing classical, not dark fantasy uses to deal with in detail. Especially <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ the messianic golem leader created by 12 golems with a head filled with good, friendly instructions, that are overlayed by the evil conspirator´s orders to kill Vetinari which stresses the king golem so much that he turns mad <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> is having pretty profound, although partly illogical arguments, that could be seen as innuendos to both faiths and colonial history. The legitimation of good violence plays in here too, because it strongly depends on the own ideology and subjective value system if someone sees a group as freedom fighters or evil rebels that should be crushed with anyone the fair, great state, and its military can offer.<br /><br />A simple reason why such controversial topics, Pratchett dares to and excels at dealing with, are rarely seen in fiction is that it needs both expertise and talent to perform it in a way that is not half baked satire, provoking, discriminating, making bad puns about horrible things, or, worst and inherent funny case, propaganda for something the writer wants to have criticized.<br />I am not sure if this is 100 percent correct, but as far as I know, Pratchett began dealing with the really heavy stuff when he already was a category mega writer who could handle plot, character, and the integration of the sophisticated, cynical meta level to reflect on human stupidity.<br /><br />Once again I haven´t read enough crime, thriller, and detective novels to get all Pratchett offers the reader, I just can´t get warm with the genre, so many characters, so less worldbuilding, if there would at least always be extreme violence, gore, and madness, but often it´s even about personal relationships and just character´s motivations and the psychological investigation, no thanks, not my cup of beer. But at least I get more out of it than out of his satires of classical literature and that´s at least something.<br /><br />Tropes show how literature is conceptualized and created and which mixture of elements makes works and genres unique: <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.php/Literature/FeetOfClay">https://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.ph...</a><br /><br />This one is added to all Pratchettian reviews:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scheibenwelt-Romane#Liste_der_Romane">https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scheibe...</a> <br />The idea of the dissected motifs rocks, highlighting the main real world inspirational elements of fiction and satire is something usually done with so called higher literature, but a much more interesting field in readable literature, as it offers the joy of reading, subtle criticism, and feeling smart all together.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11035788748418663130" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_32712667" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/32712667" />
    <a title="Joan Opyr" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/219673-joan-opyr"><img alt="Joan Opyr" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1185379784p2/219673.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/32712667?book_show_action=true">Sep 12, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joan Opyr" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joan Opyr" href="/user/show/219673-joan-opyr">Joan Opyr</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everyone</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Joan by: </span>
      Melynda Huskey
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer32712667" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5662296554283256171">What interests me most about Terry Pratchett is that he explores (with great subtlety and wit) issues that other fantasy and speculative writers only pay lip service to -- racism, sexism, identity politics. Pratchett's hero, Sam Vimes, dislikes everyone: Dwarves, trolls, werewolves, gnomes, and, most especially, vampires. He is an equal opportunity curmudgeon, but his dislikes (with the exception of vampires) are not based on the identity of "the other" as much as they are on the identity of Sam</span>
  <span id="freeText5662296554283256171" style="display:none">What interests me most about Terry Pratchett is that he explores (with great subtlety and wit) issues that other fantasy and speculative writers only pay lip service to -- racism, sexism, identity politics. Pratchett's hero, Sam Vimes, dislikes everyone: Dwarves, trolls, werewolves, gnomes, and, most especially, vampires. He is an equal opportunity curmudgeon, but his dislikes (with the exception of vampires) are not based on the identity of "the other" as much as they are on the identity of Sam Vimes. He is, as he says repeatedly, a bastard. He has no faith in the goodness of humankind. He loves his wife, he loves his son, and he loves honesty. He is as unsparing in his assessment of himself as he is aware of the faults of the world around him.<br /><br />And if this sounds like a dry assessment, then I have done Terry Pratchett a great disservice. He's one of the best writers out there, consistently smart and funny, and if you're feeling low, if the idea of a McCain-Palin administration has you breaking out in a cold sweat, then there is nothing more comforting than reading a book like <i>Feet of Clay</i>. Somehow, Sam Vimes' sour take on his Discworld makes me feel a bit better about our Earth.<br /><br />And that, my friends, is the genius of comedy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5662296554283256171" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2093927222" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2093927222" />
    <a title="Lyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn"><img alt="Lyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495541962p2/5253785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2093927222?book_show_action=true">Aug 17, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn">Lyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2093927222" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3082667449209634109">This may be the funniest Discworld novel.<br/><br/>And definitely one of the best.<br/><br/>Terry Pratchett, ever the clever turn of phrase artist, is here in rare form. This is literally chock full of puns, clichés, similes, metaphors and enough droll word play to make Nabokov choke a chicken. His easy, almost swaggering virtuosity makes this a fun read; I was literally reading the story, but waiting for and expecting his next verbal tickle.<br/><br/>It is as though when he got to this one, his 19th Discworld novel first pu</span>
  <span id="freeText3082667449209634109" style="display:none">This may be the funniest Discworld novel.<br><br>And definitely one of the best.<br><br>Terry Pratchett, ever the clever turn of phrase artist, is here in rare form. This is literally chock full of puns, clichés, similes, metaphors and enough droll word play to make Nabokov choke a chicken. His easy, almost swaggering virtuosity makes this a fun read; I was literally reading the story, but waiting for and expecting his next verbal tickle.<br><br>It is as though when he got to this one, his 19th Discworld novel first published in 1996, he had hit his stride and was making it look easy.<br><br>Captain Carrot, Sir Samuel and the members of the City Watch are investigating a murder mystery, Lord Vetinari gets mysteriously attacked, there is a strange Golem uprising and Nobby Nobbs is getting some very unexpected attention. <br><br>And Foul Old Ron takes a bath.<br><br>Just kidding.<br><br>Besides the gigglingly good word play and the Ankh-Morpork action, Sir Terry explores themes of monarchy, leadership, gender roles and what it means to be alive.<br><br>If you’ve been thinking about getting started reading the Discworld (yes, you should) this would not be a bad start.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1547343707i/26908463.jpg" width="400" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2468678218" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer4735705051302514881">More tomorrow 😀😀<br/><br/>So where do I start with this novel, easy answer is to read any of my reviews for previous Sir Terry Pratchett Discworld novels. Seriously Sir Terry manages to maintain an unbelievable standard of humour and writing whilst developing a theme that lasts for over 40 books (i think). A cast of ever increasing characters that return in different settings and in their own books.<br/>In this book we are back very firmly in Ankh-Morpork, and focussed one more on the Watch led by Commander Si</span>
  <span id="freeText4735705051302514881" style="display:none">More tomorrow 😀😀<br /><br />So where do I start with this novel, easy answer is to read any of my reviews for previous Sir Terry Pratchett Discworld novels. Seriously Sir Terry manages to maintain an unbelievable standard of humour and writing whilst developing a theme that lasts for over 40 books (i think). A cast of ever increasing characters that return in different settings and in their own books.<br />In this book we are back very firmly in Ankh-Morpork, and focussed one more on the Watch led by Commander Sir Samuel Vimes. Everything seems to be happening at once, two unrelated people are murdered, Lord Vetinari is poisoned and Vimes desparetly wants a drink. Corporal Nobbs is buttered up by the leaders of Ankh Morpork society, Captain Carrot wants to show his "Friend" Angua a dwarf Bread museum , and with crime on the increase new people are joining the Watch every day; werewolves, "lady" dwarves, experimenting with make up; and gargoyles who are good at watching.<br />Can Vimes draw all the leads and clues together before the Guilds run riot and a King is produced to rule Ankh Morpork. Amidst the chaos and with the help of a self governing Golem, Vimes races to a conclusion, but is its the right one ?<br /><br />A wonderful novel, worthy of the 5 stars</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/42884978?book_show_action=true">Jan 13, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jokoloyo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jokoloyo" href="/user/show/1902630-jokoloyo">Jokoloyo</a>

<div id="review_2649374901" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2649374901" />
    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3461034-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588663568p2/3461034.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2649374901?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/3461034-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9780753107447" href="/book/show/1141289.Feet_of_Clay">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2649374901" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1957771299321870275">This 19th <i>Discworld</i> novel is, once again, a small masterpiece. Yes, I‘m aware I sound like a broken record but it seems all of the novels have and keep this level of quality. It‘s something to behold.<br/><br/>This time, we are back in Ankh-Morpork, following Sam Vimes and the City Watch while they are trying to solve a few puzzling murders. It doesn‘t help that Vimes is a man of honour which means he‘ll keep his promise to his wife that he won‘t drink.<br/>But the City Watch, now seriously enlargened and keep</span>
  <span id="freeText1957771299321870275" style="display:none">This 19th <i>Discworld</i> novel is, once again, a small masterpiece. Yes, I‘m aware I sound like a broken record but it seems all of the novels have and keep this level of quality. It‘s something to behold.<br><br>This time, we are back in Ankh-Morpork, following Sam Vimes and the City Watch while they are trying to solve a few puzzling murders. It doesn‘t help that Vimes is a man of honour which means he‘ll keep his promise to his wife that he won‘t drink.<br>But the City Watch, now seriously enlargened and keeping to build Watch Houses, gets a charming addition: Cheery Little Bottom (a dwarf and alchemist). What better place to group together this bunch of misfits?<br>Soon, it‘s not just the murders they have to solve because Lord Vetinari has also been poisoned and even though he recovers regularly, he also keeps getting worse again shortly afterwards.<br>Not to mention that half the city is almost up in flames (literally) because of the golems. <br>But never fear, the City Watch is on the case. Or do fear ... fear very much.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1548174854i/26955785.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Artificial intelligence (well, the Discworld version of it), racism/discrimination, misogyny, religious beliefs ... and Knobby being crowned king of Ankh-Morpork (almost). That‘s one hell of a combination and thanks to the quirky characters of the watch (Colon as much as Angua, too), it‘s a wild smelly ride that works wonderfully. I kept chuckling, often even laughing out loud. From the power of words to destiny, the truly fantastic puns the author kept working in are what are standing out the most here. This installment isn’t as laugh-out-loud funny as others, but its humour is deep. Pratchett has always been great at putting a different spin at everyday expressions and conceptions but I think he put more of that in here (not least of all because the golems are all about the power of words).<br><br>My heart broke when <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Angua realized Cheery was no different than most others, on the contrary. She kept going on about not every dwarf being like all the others and wanting to be accepted as she was, but denied the possibility of the same being true about werewolves <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. Tragic. And so life-like, of course.<br>Who I always love is Carrot. How he has no sense of humour (let alone sarcasm) whatsoever and what you see is what you get (or what he says). I especially enjoyed the scene with him „making others see his point“ (both the point of his sword and the point he was getting to when explaining the problem with the golems to people). And yet, despite a man of his word being the ideal, the author realistically shows what the shortcoming of such a person would be - because being only one thing is never enough, it can always backfire. Which is why he/we need(s) the others.<br><br>And despite Ankh-Morpork not being my favourite place on the Disc, I always feel at home, here, too. Which is why I thought it was such a wonderful scene when Vimes was able to take off his perfect boots at one point and exchange them against a used pair that let him feel every pebble as that is how he knows „his“ city. <br>Or his organizer!!! That was an source of endless delight. :D<br>Small scenes but definitely not unimportant. This is one of the strengths of Sir Terry: mundane (in a fantastical sense of the world) characters or settings or even only just one sentence delivering a powerful message, making this entire world come to life in a way that set the bar impossibly high for anyone else.<br><br>Once again, Nigel Planer brought to life a diverse cast of impossible characters and I‘m already sad to know that he won‘t narrate all of them. I love the paperbacks with their funny covers but the audiobooks give the entire thing an additional flair.</["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="1957771299321870275" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1227782230" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1227782230" />
    <a title="David Sarkies" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5856247-david-sarkies"><img alt="David Sarkies" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470027282p2/5856247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1227782230?book_show_action=true">Mar 14, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Sarkies" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Sarkies" href="/user/show/5856247-david-sarkies">David Sarkies</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552142373" href="/book/show/833426.Feet_of_Clay">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">People who love satire</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to David by: </span>
      A number of friends
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5856247-david-sarkies?shelf=comedy">comedy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1227782230" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16433631230576409689"><b>The butcher, the baker ...</b><br/>23 March 2015<br/><br/>	A part of me, upon learning of Sir Terry's death, thought that it was only fitting to make the next book that I read a Discworld novel; which turned out to be this one. I won't say anything about Sir Terry here as I have already written <a href='http://www.sarkology.net/2015/03/remembering-discworld-tribute-to-terry.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>a blog post</a> on his passing and instead will just speak about this book. In fact, it turned out that so far this was one of the best discworld novels that I have read (and that is saying something since there are quite a few</span>
  <span id="freeText16433631230576409689" style="display:none"><b>The butcher, the baker ...</b><br />23 March 2015<br /><br />	A part of me, upon learning of Sir Terry's death, thought that it was only fitting to make the next book that I read a Discworld novel; which turned out to be this one. I won't say anything about Sir Terry here as I have already written <a href="http://www.sarkology.net/2015/03/remembering-discworld-tribute-to-terry.html" rel="nofollow noopener">a blog post</a> on his passing and instead will just speak about this book. In fact, it turned out that so far this was one of the best discworld novels that I have read (and that is saying something since there are quite a few contenders out there, and it also goes to show how great a writer he is if he can still hold my interest this far into the series).<br />	At first Feet of Clay reminded me a lot of the movie I, Robot – you know the one where Will Smith discovers that the robots that were created to serve humanity are actually planning a takeover? The problem is that this book was written quite a few years before Will Smith took to the stage playing a cop in a movie that give the term 'loosely based' a completely new definition (and for those who have not seen the film, but read the book, the only similarities between the two is that they have robots in them – well, not quite, but you get the picture).<br />	Mind you Feet of Clay is definitely a 'cop novel'. I would say a 'cop movie' but it is not actually a movie – it is a novel, but I guess the term is sort of transferable. Okay, it is partly a detective novel because there have been a couple of murders, as well as an assassination attempt (isn't if funny that if a person is unimportant then it is a murder, but if they are important they are assassinated – why can't I be assassinated, it would be much better than being murdered – at least in my opinion), and Captain Vimes is trying to find the person behind it. So Vimes (and the rest of the City Watch) goes out to investige the situation - it isn't as if it is an Agatha Christie novel: you know, set in a static place (unless you consider Anhk-Morpork a static place, but for some reason I really don't think that actually counts) where there are a bunch of culprits and you are supposed to work out the guilty party before the author gives it all away. Okay, Sir Terry does give us some clues, such as the Butcher, the Baker, and the Candlestick Maker, but that doesn't necessarily tell you how the poison was administered (and it is quite clever in that regards).<br />	So, what has this to do with robots, you ask? Well, the book is about golems - you know those creatures made of clay (or whatever non-living material may be at hand, including corpses, but in Discworld they are made of clay) who do other people's bidding. Well, they do play a role in this book, and they are effectively the magical version of a robot. Actually, to our modern mind, golems really seem to be a magical version of a robot, and Sir Terry plays that idea up to no end. What is interesting is that this idea goes back to the Ancient Greeks (though I can't quite remember which myth it was) where one of the gods, or was it a mortal, I can't remember which off the top of my head, did create something that was remarkably similar to a robot. There is also another legend, from the Jewish Quarter of Prague, where a golem was created to protect the Jews from their enemies (a legend that I discovered when I actually visited that city where I picked up a book called <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2250486.The_Prague_Golem" title="The Prague Golem by Vitalis" rel="nofollow noopener">The Prague Golem</a>).<br />	Interestingly enough he is also playing up the fears of automation in this book as well. We, or at least the working class, don't like robots because they take away our jobs. However these robots are so much more efficient, and faster, than any human could ever be. In Discworld we are told that golems do not eat, sleep, or require any maintenance so they are much better than the ordinary worker. However, the problem is that they scare people, they scare people because they are so life like, yet they are unliving. In a sense they have a body, and a mind, but they have no soul. This is probably why I connected it with I Robot (the movie, not the book) because, in many ways, the robots were so creepy because of that very thing. In every sense of the word they were alive, but in reality they were not. Okay, they aren't undead – at least undead beings were at one stage alive, but they are not exactly living either.<br />	Anyway, I should probably finish this review off here, though I should say that I really enjoyed this book, and am compelled to continue reading his books right down to the final one. I'm not sure if I will get to the last one, but at least I will try.<br />	Also, for those who are interested, you can find my tribute to Terry Pratchett <a href="http://www.sarkology.net/2015/03/remembering-discworld-tribute-to-terry.html" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a> (and sorry, it may not be as fancy as hiding it in computer code, but I felt that I should probably write one anyway).</span>
  <a data-text-id="16433631230576409689" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1278525214?book_show_action=true">May 12, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard" href="/user/show/8386404-richard">Richard</a>
      </span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1278525214" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10874379169858722802">8.5/10<br/> <br/>Another great entry into the Discworld series overall and makes it hard for me not to think I made an error not starting the Watch sub-series as my first foray into Discworld. The 3 novels I’ve read in this sub-series have probably all been better than the other Discworld books I’ve read previously (maybe exclude “Going Postal” in that). It may be that I have a better affinity with crime driven stories so I can get on board with the plot quicker but I just think it might have something to</span>
  <span id="freeText10874379169858722802" style="display:none">8.5/10<br /> <br />Another great entry into the Discworld series overall and makes it hard for me not to think I made an error not starting the Watch sub-series as my first foray into Discworld. The 3 novels I’ve read in this sub-series have probably all been better than the other Discworld books I’ve read previously (maybe exclude “Going Postal” in that). It may be that I have a better affinity with crime driven stories so I can get on board with the plot quicker but I just think it might have something to do with the cast of characters which add to the story.<br /> <br />With this being the 3rd entry in the sub-series the main set of characters are well fleshed out by now with the reader knowing their nuances and this means more fun can be had with them and also allow some of the side characters to have more of an input and make for some of the more humorous moments in the novel. Detritius is a standout side character here and Cheery Littlebottom adds another dynamic with the <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[female perspective of <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> life in the Watch. I enjoyed the way Terry Pratchett looks at, and pokes fun at, sexism in institutions in this one. Some of the more humorous moments in the whole Discworld series come from the attitudes of people which are clearly a reflection on the attitudes of people from our world but with Pratchett’s unique humour added to it. <br /> <br />The story is pretty much a “whodunit” if you can really call it that. It’s driven along by this but there is so much going on here to enjoy it sort of felt like a side part of the book as a whole. It works well though as I had no idea who the mystery person was and when it was revealed it lead to a huge “of course!” moment from me. Well done to Terry for pulling the wool over my eyes. It made me feel like Vimes in that I can’t see something right in front of me.<br /> <br />This also brings me to the moments between Vimes &amp; Vetinari. By far the standout moments of the book for me comes in the interactions of these two. Both are wily in their ways but both get the job done even if it is by manipulation of the other. I look forward to these moments more and more.<br /> <br />Overall a great book, well worth a read but I’d recommend starting with “Guards! Guards!” first as there is definitely more to appreciate having read about these characters beforehand. If the upwards trajectory of these stories continue I can’t see how the next one won’t reach the elusive 5 star rating, the last 3 have only just missed out. I guess there is only one way to find out. Bring on the next one!!<br /> <br />If you like this try: ”Going Postal” by Terry Pratchett</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer989792061" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer14327020226409508641">2008 January 1<br/><br/>It probably shouldn't be allowed for a writer to have this much fun with a mystery story.<br/><br/>***<br/><br/>2014 August 20<br/><br/>More mysteries: who is poisoning Vetinari, and, more importantly, how? Who killed the two old men? Why are the golems suddenly acting odd?<br/><br/>Vimes is hard at work on the first question, Carrot is looking into the second and third. Vital assistance is provided by Angua, Detritus, Colon, and Nobbs. Also, there's a new member of the watch, formerly of the alchemists, Cheery Littlebot</span>
  <span id="freeText14327020226409508641" style="display:none">2008 January 1<br /><br />It probably shouldn't be allowed for a writer to have this much fun with a mystery story.<br /><br />***<br /><br />2014 August 20<br /><br />More mysteries: who is poisoning Vetinari, and, more importantly, how? Who killed the two old men? Why are the golems suddenly acting odd?<br /><br />Vimes is hard at work on the first question, Carrot is looking into the second and third. Vital assistance is provided by Angua, Detritus, Colon, and Nobbs. Also, there's a new member of the watch, formerly of the alchemists, Cheery Littlebottom. His job is to run the forensics, His role in the book is to open up the questions of minority members of the force and gender identity.<br /><br />Pratchett uses various species to show how everyone has prejudices and how getting to know a member of a species does a great deal to dispel them. Well, it does if one is a good person. The bad guys persist in cruel stereotyping regardless.<br /><br />There's been some research recently into reader identification (with characters in the Harry Potter series) and what effect that has upon readers' empathy. Pratchett uses magical creatures just as Rowling does, to continually push at the definition of "human" and to demonstrate how people can behave morally without the imposition of gods or religions. Pratchett's funnier, though.<br /><br />Personal copy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14327020226409508641" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1259302349?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lindsay" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lindsay" href="/user/show/10326608-lindsay">Lindsay</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

  <span id="freeText2778436223319307795" style="display:none">Part of the Pratchett reread with the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/162270-specfic-buddy-reads" rel="nofollow noopener">SpecFic Buddy Reads</a> group in 2018.<br /><br />Back to the Watch sub-series and the introduction of Cheery Littlebottom to the force, the new Watch alchemist/forensics corporal and female dwarf. <div id="review_1257088958" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1257088958" />
    <a title="Tanya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15698555-tanya"><img alt="Tanya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602837639p2/15698555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1257088958?book_show_action=true">Jan 05, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tanya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tanya" href="/user/show/15698555-tanya">Tanya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781473200241" href="/book/show/20494023-feet-of-clay">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1257088958" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1899511229771730393">This book was just delightful, cemented the City Watch as my favorite Discworld subseries, and marked the first novel where I wasn't only riding along for the chuckles and veiled social commentary, but where I was really and truly invested in the murder mystery Pratchett spun. Well, a mystery of a different kind—Commander Vimes isn't only determined to find out whodunit, but howdunit too, and we see the introduction of forensics in Ankh-Morpork.<br/><br/><blockquote><i>“Samuel Vimes dreamed about Clues. He had a jaundic</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText1899511229771730393" style="display:none">This book was just delightful, cemented the City Watch as my favorite Discworld subseries, and marked the first novel where I wasn't only riding along for the chuckles and veiled social commentary, but where I was really and truly invested in the murder mystery Pratchett spun. Well, a mystery of a different kind—Commander Vimes isn't only determined to find out whodunit, but howdunit too, and we see the introduction of forensics in Ankh-Morpork.<br /><br /><blockquote><i>“Samuel Vimes dreamed about Clues. He had a jaundiced view of Clues. He instinctively distrusted them. They got in the way. And he distrusted the kind of person who’d take one look at another man and say in a lordly voice to his companion, </i>“Ah, my dear sir, I can tell you nothing except that he is a left-handed stonemason who has spent some years in the merchant navy and has recently fallen on hard times,” <i>and then unroll a lot of supercilious commentary about calluses and stance and the state of a man’s boots, when exactly the same comments could apply to a man who was wearing his old clothes because he’d been doing a spot of home bricklaying for a new barbecue pit, and had been tattooed once when he was drunk and seventeen and in fact got seasick on a wet pavement. What arrogance! What an insult to the rich and chaotic variety of the human experience!”</i></blockquote><br /><br />The characters have been sufficiently fleshed out in the previous books, so we get right down to the good stuff. Some plot patterns resemble those of previous novels in the sub-series, but it's been vastly improved and works seamlessly, which is one of the things I often have to lament—Pratchett often spins multiple diverging plot-lines, but ultimately fails at properly tying them up, which makes for a choppy reading experience... but that wasn't an issue in <i>Feet of Clay</i> at all.<br /><br />As the title suggests, this novel sees the introduction of the Golem, which allowed him to expand on the issues of discrimination he's explored before with trolls, dwarfs, werewolves and other "minorities", this time expanding the question to what makes a person a person in the first place, on top of themes regarding gender, monarchy, stereotypes, and morality without religion. And all of it is hilarious. I usually find myself smiling at his witty word-play and jokes, but this one had me snort-laughing.<br /><br />I've said it before, but it bears repeating: The City Watch books are my favorite, and the perfect place to start for anyone interested in starting their Discworld journey.<br /><br />————— <br /><br />My other <i>Discworld</i> reviews by sub-series (work in progress):<br /><br /><i>Rincewind / Unseen University Wizards:</i> The Colour of Magic · The Light Fantastic · Sourcery · Eric · <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1257088930" rel="nofollow noopener">Interesting Times</a> · <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1260017230" rel="nofollow noopener">The Last Continent</a> · The Last Hero · Unseen Academicals <br /><br /><i>Witches:</i> Equal Rites · Wyrd Systers · Witches Abroad · Lords and Ladies · <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1257088937" rel="nofollow noopener">Maskerade</a> · <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1260017315" rel="nofollow noopener">Carpe Jugulum</a> <br /><br /><i>Death</i>: Mort · Reaper Man · <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1257088890" rel="nofollow noopener">Soul Music</a> · <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1260017160" rel="nofollow noopener">Hogfather</a> · Thief of Time <br /><br /><i>Gods:</i> Pyramids · Small Gods<br /><br /><i>City Watch:</i> Guards! Guards! · <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1257088865" rel="nofollow noopener">Men At Arms</a> · <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1257088958" rel="nofollow noopener">Feet of Clay</a> · <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1260017177" rel="nofollow noopener">Jingo</a> · <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1260017366" rel="nofollow noopener">The Fifth Elephant</a> · Night Watch · Thud! · Snuff<br /><br /><i>Industrial Revolution / Moist von Lipwig:</i> Moving Pictures · The Truth · Monstrous Regiment · Going Postal · Making Money · Raising Steam<br /><br /><i>Tiffany Aching:</i> The Wee Free Men · A Hat Full of Sky · Wintersmith · I Shall Wear Midnight · The Sheperd's Crown</span>

Mysteries pupped."<br /><br />"It was easy to be a vegetarian by day. It was preventing yourself becoming a humanitarian at night that took the real effort." (concerning Angua, a werewolf)<br /><br />"They think they want good government and justice for all, Vimes, yet what it is they really crave, deep in their hearts? Only that things go on as normal and tomorrow is pretty much like today."<br /><br />"If you were going to be successful in the world of crime, you needed a reputation for honesty.<br /><br />"You had to be on the side of underdogs because they weren't overdogs."<br /><br />"No thought was involved. It is the ancient instinct of terriers and policemen to chase anything that runs away."<br /><br />"With the buzz of other people around him, he didn't seem to get in the way of his own thoughts."<br /><br />"I'm worried and confused. So the first rule in the book is to spread it around."<br /><br />"No one can be as sane as he is without being mad."<br /><br />"The royal lines had died out throught interbreeding so intensively that the last king kept trying to breed with himself."<br /><br />"A horrible apprehension had stolen over him that almost all the answers were in place now, if only he could work out the questions."<br /><br />"When you've made up your mind to shout out who you are to the world, it's a relief to know that you can do it in a whisper."<br /><br />"Someone has to be very complex indeed to be as simple as Carrot."<br /><br />"The common people? They're nothing special. They're no different from the rich and powerful except they've got no money or power. But the law should be there to balance things up a bit. So I suppose I've got to be on their side."<br /><br />"Atheism is a religious position. Indeed a true atheist thinks of the gods constantly, albeit in terms of denial. Therefore, atheism is a form of belief. If the atheist really did not believe he or she would not bother to deny."<br /><br />"You say to people 'throw off your chains' and they make new chains for themselves."<br /><br /> <br /><br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9134844253053232947">Awwwwww. I didn't expect to feel so heartwarmed over a Discworld book, but damn if this book didn't me feel all fuzzy inside. I'm just such a sucker for stories about, well, what this book is about. SPOILERSSS.<br/><br/>So this is a City Watch book, and that means several things: Captain Samuel Vimes doing good deeds and being very grouchy about it, Carrot being overly literal and good at his job while everyone likes him with no effort on his part whatsoever, and the other members of the Watch being also </span>
  <span id="freeText9134844253053232947" style="display:none">Awwwwww. I didn't expect to feel so heartwarmed over a Discworld book, but damn if this book didn't me feel all fuzzy inside. I'm just such a sucker for stories about, well, what this book is about. SPOILERSSS.<br /><br />So this is a City Watch book, and that means several things: Captain Samuel Vimes doing good deeds and being very grouchy about it, Carrot being overly literal and good at his job while everyone likes him with no effort on his part whatsoever, and the other members of the Watch being also good at their jobs, except almost by accident. This time, they've got to track down a murderer who seems to be killing people for seemingly no reason, another who is trying to assassinate the Patrician, and all while the city's golems are acting very, very strangely. <br /><br />As with all of Pratchett's books, you're not really sure what you're in for at the beginning of the book, and it all seems to be pretty random, but then it starts to come together, and you're like, oh. All of a sudden you're all wrapped up in a story about class concerns, what it means to be a public servant, what it means to be a person, and also, a story about what if Nobby Nobbs was actually a secret earl and has to eat all this fancy food.<br /><br />All in all, yes to this book, would read again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9134844253053232947" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3365640178179935989"><b>5 Words: Power, control, deception, life, police.</b><br/><br/>This was an excellent audiobook - I think that Nigel Planer did another fantastic job narrating the story.<br/><br/>Feet of Clay felt a lot more More (if that makes sense?) in that everything felt fully developed, and the characters had more depth than in the previous books. I feel like I actually know the characters, and I honestly love Vimes and his pettiness.</span>

<div id="review_636464413" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/636464413" />
    <a title="Olga Godim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7438487-olga-godim"><img alt="Olga Godim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442639299p2/7438487.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/636464413?book_show_action=true">Jun 08, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Olga Godim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Olga Godim" href="/user/show/7438487-olga-godim">Olga Godim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552142373" href="/book/show/833426.Feet_of_Clay">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7438487-olga-godim?shelf=fantasy-scifi">fantasy-scifi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer636464413" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12724169367674911668">This is a mystery: several murders have been committed, and the Watch of Ankh-Morpork is investigating. The Watch commander, Sam Vimes, has an additional problem: someone is poisoning Lord Vetinary, the Patrician. As both investigations proceed, the author parades in front of the readers a score of characters, each one faultier that the others. <br/>In the lead of that parade is the protagonist Sam Vimes: a cynical policeman, a recovering alcoholic, and one of the few good guys in this predominantly </span>
  <span id="freeText12724169367674911668" style="display:none">This is a mystery: several murders have been committed, and the Watch of Ankh-Morpork is investigating. The Watch commander, Sam Vimes, has an additional problem: someone is poisoning Lord Vetinary, the Patrician. As both investigations proceed, the author parades in front of the readers a score of characters, each one faultier that the others. <br />In the lead of that parade is the protagonist Sam Vimes: a cynical policeman, a recovering alcoholic, and one of the few good guys in this predominantly gloomy book. To add to his other qualities – shrewdness and basic honesty, so rare in the city – Vimes is also an equal opportunity employer. His police force includes not only humans but also a werewolf, a dwarf, a gargoyle, several trolls, and too many fools to mention. <br />Stupidity seems to be very dense in the police force of Ankh-Morpork, almost like a contagious disease, touching everyone indiscriminately and spreading like wildfire. As a result, everyone is sneezing and coughing (aka acting like an idiot) in his own inimitable way. There is no stopping this malady, so common for all the races on Discworld (and Earth). Although some people succumb to harsher symptoms than others, few recover, at least few males. The exceptions are females: the vegetarian werewolf, Constable Angua, and the dwarf alchemist Cheery, the lone CSI of the Watch. <br />As the police searches for the murderers and the poisoners, Pratchett paints a broad picture of the city: the corporate moguls and the religious fanatics, the slaves and the nobles. Everyone is scheming, even the golems (robots), and the emerging story veers towards the macabre, with multiple blood and gore episodes, rampant prejudice, and undisguised racism. The author doesn’t spare anyone in his morbid insights and witty observations. His city feels like a black caricature of our own society, a grotesque mirror where everyone can recognize himself… if he is brave enough. The recognition had its rewards too: sometime I laughed so hard, my eyes teared up.<br />I must admit that there are a couple of problems in this book. One is the author’s tendency to write phonetically, whenever one of his ‘uneducated’ or ‘uncultured’ characters speaks. The result is the atrocious spelling of the above-mentioned speech chunks, so bad it was hard to understand sometimes. <br />The second problem concerns the start of the story. It is slow and clunky, comprised of too many disjointed fragments and introducing too many characters too fast. Fortunately, as the plot rolls on, it smoothes out, and Pratchett’s inevitable witty quotes worm their way into the text. As is common for the Discworld books, too many quotes are worth repeating, but a few I just couldn’t resist.<br /><br />Vimes’s contemplations of the follies of human nature: <blockquote>
  <i>Royalty was like dandelions. No matter how many heads you chopped off, the roots were still there, underground, waiting to spring up again. <br />It seemed to be a chronic disease. It was as if even the most intelligent person had this little blank spot in their heads where someone had written: ‘Kings. What a good idea.’ Whoever had created humanity had left in a major design flaw. It was its tendency to bend at the knees.</i>
</blockquote> <br />Constable Visit, a religious zealot who usually plagues Vimes with spiritual brochures and unasked-for sermons, arrives to report on the investigation: <blockquote>
  <i>‘Oh. Yes. Come on into my office.’ Vimes relaxed. This wasn’t going to be another one of those painful conversations about the state of his soul and the necessity of giving it a wash and brush-up before eternal damnation set in. This was going to be about something <b>important</b>. </i>
</blockquote><br />When Angua tells Vimes of a secret meeting of the golems, he responds: <blockquote>
  <i>‘You like the idea of them having <b>secrets</b>? I mean, good grief, trolls and dwarfs, fine, even the undead are alive in a way, even if it is a bloody awful way’ – Vimes caught Angua’s eye and went on – ‘for the most part. But these things? They’re just things that do work. It’s like having a bunch of shovels meeting for a chat!’ </i>
</blockquote><br />Note: the <b>highlights</b> belong to the author.<br />Recommended to every courageous creature out there, anyone willing to look into a Pratchett-shaped mirror, be it a troll, a dwarf, or a human. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12724169367674911668" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1799367477" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1799367477" />
    <a title="YouKneeK" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25923230-youkneek"><img alt="YouKneeK" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1386369592p2/25923230.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1799367477?book_show_action=true">Nov 03, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="YouKneeK" class="user" itemprop="url" name="YouKneeK" href="/user/show/25923230-youkneek">YouKneeK</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25923230-youkneek?shelf=completed-series">completed-series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25923230-youkneek?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1799367477" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17564616075931615020"><i>Feet of Clay</i> is the third book in the <i>Watch</i> subseries of <i>Discworld</i>. It’s also the <i>Guards</i> book that I’ve enjoyed the most so far. In fact, it may even have given my previous favorite <i>Discworld</i> book, <i>Wyrd Sisters</i>, some competition.<br/><br/>I was worried at first that this book would rehash old ground with the “let’s get ourselves a puppet king” thing. There’s a bit of that, and it’s an important aspect of the story, but it doesn’t overpower the story and it has a more amusing twist than in previous books. </span>
  <span id="freeText17564616075931615020" style="display:none"><i>Feet of Clay</i> is the third book in the <i>Watch</i> subseries of <i>Discworld</i>. It’s also the <i>Guards</i> book that I’ve enjoyed the most so far. In fact, it may even have given my previous favorite <i>Discworld</i> book, <i>Wyrd Sisters</i>, some competition.<br /><br />I was worried at first that this book would rehash old ground with the “let’s get ourselves a puppet king” thing. There’s a bit of that, and it’s an important aspect of the story, but it doesn’t overpower the story and it has a more amusing twist than in previous books. In general, I thought this plot was one of the better ones. Parts of it actually kept me guessing, and I was truly interested in finding out what would happen next as opposed to just sitting back, going along for the ride, and enjoying the humor like I normally do when I read these books. <br /><br />Character-wise, I enjoyed the mish-mash of characters and their different backgrounds and amusing perspectives. I enjoyed the introduction of that in the second <i>Watch</i> book, and things got even livelier in this book. Even Vimes seemed less annoying to me than he had in the previous books. Also, his pocket organizer was hilarious. This seemed like one of Pratchett's better ones in terms of humor. The books are all funny, but this one had more parts that made me laugh out loud. <br /><br />I’m not sure many people would appreciate the corniness of this insult, but I loved this: <i>“… a ding-a-ling so big he’d been upgraded to a clang-a-lang”</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17564616075931615020" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1970166357" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1970166357" />
    <a title="Tony" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/66855950-tony"><img alt="Tony" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1541836618p2/66855950.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1970166357?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tony" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tony" href="/user/show/66855950-tony">Tony</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

<div id="review_1390900162" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1390900162" />
    <a title="Chris Boulton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21130273-chris-boulton"><img alt="Chris Boulton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1510481418p2/21130273.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1390900162?book_show_action=true">Sep 13, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris Boulton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris Boulton" href="/user/show/21130273-chris-boulton">Chris Boulton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552142373" href="/book/show/833426.Feet_of_Clay">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1390900162" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11450968061949668044">I have to confess that originally this wasn't one of my favourites out of 'The Watch' books.. just didn't seem to click like the others did but this time round, I really enjoyed it. Maybe writing these wee reviews made me appreciate it a bit more because I knew I would have to write something about it afterwards!<br/><br/>There's a bit of this, towards the end, that made me think..<br/><br/>'The thought occurs, sir, that if Commander Vimes did not exist you would have had to invent him.' <br/>'You know, Drumknott, I rat</span>
  <span id="freeText11450968061949668044" style="display:none">I have to confess that originally this wasn't one of my favourites out of 'The Watch' books.. just didn't seem to click like the others did but this time round, I really enjoyed it. Maybe writing these wee reviews made me appreciate it a bit more because I knew I would have to write something about it afterwards!<br /><br />There's a bit of this, towards the end, that made me think..<br /><br />'The thought occurs, sir, that if Commander Vimes did not exist you would have had to invent him.' <br />'You know, Drumknott, I rather think I did.'<br /><br />.. I've got it into my head that Vetinari is 'grooming' Vimes to be his successor. I don't know whether this is a theory that I've come to on my own or whether I've read it in one of the books and I've just forgotten that I read it somewhere. As I read that quoted bit above and thought about him being his successor .. an idea formed in me ole noggin' that, in Vetinari's mind, Vimes is the embodiment of a part of his personality that he can't show... the idea of 'fairness'. Throughout this book, you have people describe what Vetinari is all about.. how he's come in, apparently a decade before this story, and made Ankh-Morpork work by ironing out the guilds and the best line of 'if you've got to have crime, you might as well make it organised crime' (or something along those lines). He also makes his position more secure by making the city leaders reliant on him.. sure, life is hard but just imagine how hard it would be without him, etc etc.<br /><br />But to get all this to work, he can't be fair and just. He needs to be hard, he needs to be sarcastic and ironic. He needs to be the tyrant that we all know and love. Vimes, on the other hand, is the embodiment of justice and fair play for the common man (and the city in general, I suppose). He fights for what's right and he fights for the law (even if he's making it up most of the time). If you think about the ending of each of these books so far.. Vimes has usually caused a stink, the city leaders are annoyed with him, he's disobeyed direct orders from Vetinari, he's thrown someone in the dungeons on very little evidence (even though everyone know's he's got the right person) and Vetinari always gives him a bonus, a raise or a new dartboard because he knows that Vimes has done what was right and if he seems to have forgiven Vimes then the city leaders will forgive him, because well they have no choice, but if he seems to be getting soft by doing these things (causing a stink, fair play and all that) himself then there's a chance that people will stand up to him and remove him from power, because they lost their fear of him, and the city will come to a grinding halt. If you put them both in one of those ... uh... is it Venn diagrams? They'd over-lap in the 'making Ankh-Morpork work' in the middle. I think that, possibly, if Sir Terry was still with us and able to continue writing then maybe, just maybe, Vimes would be the leader - once he took over - that would be perfect for Ankh-Morpork.. a fair mixture of both Vetinari and Vimes' style.<br /><br />This is probably all bollo--nonsense but it's sometimes good to just vomit out a load of nonsense occasionally.<br /><br />Another history book next... and then after that.. my all time favourite Watch story.. :D</span>
  <a data-text-id="11450968061949668044" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_321304906" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/321304906" />
    <a title="Winonah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9029100-winonah"><img alt="Winonah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1335306616p2/9029100.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/321304906?book_show_action=true">Apr 29, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Winonah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Winonah" href="/user/show/9029100-winonah">Winonah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780061052507" href="/book/show/618150.Feet_of_Clay">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9029100-winonah?shelf=favorite">favorite</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer321304906" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15528840377716450076">This is one of Pratchett's books that gained him the reputation for straying from science fiction into literature. Most of the depth escaped me when I first read it 12 or so years ago, both because I was younger and because it was the first Discworld book I read. After years of feeling that popular fiction was too shallow and most of the classics were too difficult, a friend encouraged me to read this. I expected a YA book with cleverer pop culture references. Within two pages, though, it was cl</span>
  <span id="freeText15528840377716450076" style="display:none">This is one of Pratchett's books that gained him the reputation for straying from science fiction into literature. Most of the depth escaped me when I first read it 12 or so years ago, both because I was younger and because it was the first Discworld book I read. After years of feeling that popular fiction was too shallow and most of the classics were too difficult, a friend encouraged me to read this. I expected a YA book with cleverer pop culture references. Within two pages, though, it was clear that my dreams of satire with heart had been fulfilled. I asked to borrow anything else by Terry Pratchett as soon as I'd finished it. It was hard to explain what was so enthralling about it back then. The concept was deeper than I'd expected. It was too deep for my younger self to fully absorb. He was playing with the idea of artificial life. Golems strike the same nerve that robots do, having the ability to be programmed but not to make decisions. The people of Disworld were afraid of them because they couldn't predict what they might do if something went wrong, or if they somehow gained consciousness. I delighted in Pratchett's story of a Golem gaining self-awareness and subsequently declaring himself an atheist. This struck me as the best irony-that the only monsters were the people who wanted to stop Dorfl from his non-violent rampage against them for their treatment of golems and animals. The eclectic people/person-shaped members of the watch used their idiosyncracies to help Dorfl and therefore everyone. Pratchett became my hero. In time, I've come to understand even more of what he understood when he wrote this book. What defines humanity is debated apart from consciousness, so if consciousness can be created-and reject its maker's will-what are we? I like to think I'd stuff a butcher with onion and sage if he treated me like machinery too. Using Golems made the point that definitions of free will and life have been debated for a long time and will probably go on long after our PCs criticize our photo edits. I just wish Pratchett would be with us long enough to see the issue evolve further.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15528840377716450076" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
